    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12205024339011705725">From the National Book Award–winning, bestselling author of <i>Tree of Smoke</i> comes a provocative thriller set in the American West. <i>Nobody Move</i>, which first appeared in the pages of Playboy, is the story of an assortment of lowlifes in Bakersfield, California, and their cat-and-mouse game over $2.3 million. Touched by echoes of Raymond Chandler and Dashiell Hammett, <i>Nobody Mo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12205024339011705725" style="display:none">From the National Book Award–winning, bestselling author of <i>Tree of Smoke</i> comes a provocative thriller set in the American West. <i>Nobody Move</i>, which first appeared in the pages of Playboy, is the story of an assortment of lowlifes in Bakersfield, California, and their cat-and-mouse game over $2.3 million. Touched by echoes of Raymond Chandler and Dashiell Hammett, <i>Nobody Move</i> is at once an homage to and a variation on literary form. It salutes one of our most enduring and popular genres—the American crime novel—but with a grisly humor and outrageousness that are Denis Johnson’s own. Sexy, suspenseful, and above all entertaining, <i>Nobody Move</i> shows one of our greatest novelists at his versatile best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12205024339011705725" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_66335623" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66335623" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419287-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289534648p2/419287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/66335623?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/419287-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jessica by: </span>
      donald, et al
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=crazy-ladies">crazy-ladies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=crime-and-punishment">crime-and-punishment</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=california-%C3%BCber-alles">california-über-alles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/419287-jessica?shelf=substance-related-disorders">substance-related-disorders</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66335623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15486194654047380900">Okay, so I'll take back my fifteen-year-old pronouncement based on nothing but adolescent prejudice, and finally admit it: Denis Johnson is a really good writer. I wish I could write like Denis Johnson, unless that'd mean I'd also have to dress like Denis Johnson, and start going around in the off-channel-quickly-canceled-nineties-cop-show style purple blazer he's wearing in his author's photo. In that case I guess I'll just go on writing like me, and live with the depressing and thoroughly unex</span>
  <span id="freeText15486194654047380900" style="display:none">Okay, so I'll take back my fifteen-year-old pronouncement based on nothing but adolescent prejudice, and finally admit it: Denis Johnson is a really good writer. I wish I could write like Denis Johnson, unless that'd mean I'd also have to dress like Denis Johnson, and start going around in the off-channel-quickly-canceled-nineties-cop-show style purple blazer he's wearing in his author's photo. In that case I guess I'll just go on writing like me, and live with the depressing and thoroughly unexciting consequences of this.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book! I don't see how anyone who doesn't like any crime fiction at least a little bit wouldn't. It made me think of that trend over the past few years in trendy upscale restaurants, of presenting lowbrow American classics in somewhat gussied-up forms. You know, like White Castle-style Sliders but with free-range Niman Ranch beef, or S'mores made with organic chocolate and home-baked graham crackers and locally-grown marshmallows.... you know? <i>Nobody Move</i> was free-range, Farmers' Market Elmore Leonard, which is not at all to say Johnson's a better writer than Leonard, just that this felt like it cost a lot more per plate.<br /><br />Or per word, which it did, and that brings me to the problem: PORTION SIZE, DENIS! I know short can be sweet and that size shouldn't matter, but if I'd paid the $23 for this advertised "novel" instead of getting it from the library, I would've been <i>pissed.</i> I would've felt like I'd just been cut out of my portion of an embezzled $2.3 million, and hopped in my distinctive automobile to embark on a mad and ruthless quest for revenge.... I started the book this morning on my commute to work, and I finished it before I reached my station on the way home this evening. Okay, so to be fair I did a good amount of traveling around at work on the train today, but still. This is the personal preference of a helpless blabbermouth who obviously believes that more is more despite evidence against it, but I didn't feel I spent quite enough time with these characters to get to know them how I wanted to, which is sort of a compliment.<br /><br />Anyway, despite all my whining, I did think this was great. I wouldn't pay for it, though. Borrow this from the library, and bring an extra book along in case you finish so you don't get stranded. Have you ever gone out to a fancy restaurant then stopped by the Dairy Queen for a Blizzard later because you were still hungry? That might happen here. Still, it tastes terrific while you're eating, and I do recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15486194654047380900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer4632564141772066474">Two word review: Very meh. <br/><br/>Unexpected side effect during or after reading: Urge to read a better crime novel. <br/><br/>New thing I learned from reading this book: Singers in barber shop quartets may not be as wholesome as they seem.<br/><br/>General observations: The jacket notes on this book gave me heartburn right off the bat:<br/><br/>"Touched by echoes of Raymond Chandler and Dashiell Hammett, Nobody Move is at once an homage to and a variation on literary form. It salutes one of our most enduring and popular genres—the</span>
  <span id="freeText4632564141772066474" style="display:none">Two word review: Very meh. <br /><br />Unexpected side effect during or after reading: Urge to read a better crime novel. <br /><br />New thing I learned from reading this book: Singers in barber shop quartets may not be as wholesome as they seem.<br /><br />General observations: The jacket notes on this book gave me heartburn right off the bat:<br /><br />"Touched by echoes of Raymond Chandler and Dashiell Hammett, Nobody Move is at once an homage to and a variation on literary form. It salutes one of our most enduring and popular genres—the American crime novel—but does so with a grisly humor and outrageousness that are Denis Johnson’s own."<br /><br />In other words, Denis Johnson is a National Book Award winner and would never lower himself to writing a crime novel. But if he does an homage, that's cool.<br /><br />Frankly, he should stick to the serious 'liter-a-ture'. Because this isn't that great. It starts off with a lot of promise and some great characters, but I didn't get 'echoes of Raymond Chandler and Dashiell Hammett'. To me, it read like Johnson was flipping channels one day, caught a rerun of Pulp Fiction or maybe Things To Do In Denver When You're Dead, and decided he could throw together a couple of hundred pages like that and call it an homage.<br /><br />I had a lot more fun and got more satisfaction out of reading recent Hard Case Crime novels by writers who didn't act like they were slumming.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56789885" />
    <a title="D. Pow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow"><img alt="D. Pow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339098707p2/2182799.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56789885?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D. Pow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="D. Pow" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow">D. Pow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2182799-d-pow?shelf=noir-crime-murder">noir-crime-murder</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56789885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8311022327416648557">This was a damn good read. It combines the cheap thrills and casual violence of a typical noir with the perfectly crafted sentences and deft-characterization found in a literary novel. The book is brief, maybe too brief(my ARC copy was 195 pages set in giant type with massive swathes of blank bordering) but maybe that’s a good thing after Johnson’s most recent door stop of a novel `Tree of Smoke’, a massive, tedious lunge at the great American Novel, that to me, failed miserably. `Nobody Moves’ </span>
  <span id="freeText8311022327416648557" style="display:none">This was a damn good read. It combines the cheap thrills and casual violence of a typical noir with the perfectly crafted sentences and deft-characterization found in a literary novel. The book is brief, maybe too brief(my ARC copy was 195 pages set in giant type with massive swathes of blank bordering) but maybe that’s a good thing after Johnson’s most recent door stop of a novel `Tree of Smoke’, a massive, tedious lunge at the great American Novel, that to me, failed miserably. `Nobody Moves’ is tight, concise and is not weighted down with the heavy freight of `Serious Literature’(say it with me now in a stuffy Boston Brahmin accent-think of John Houseman in ‘The Paper Chase’) that `Smoke’ labored under. And while the new book is a light entertainment it also is saturated with off-handedly brilliant prose, characters that come alive with a few deft strokes and the sort of action-packed plotting and explosive dénouement that less talented thriller writers would give up their collective left nuts for. Highly recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8311022327416648557" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_345094131" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345094131" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345094131?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=guy-lit">guy-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=pulp-fiction">pulp-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345094131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16551817566972004594">Bakerfield, California. <b>Jimmy Luntz</b> works for <b>Gambol</b> who in turn works for <b>Juarez.</b> Luntz loves a beautiful alcoholic Indian girl called <b>Anita Desilvera.</b> They are all petty thieves. One time, they got a big haul from a bank: $2.5M. This book is about greed and who should get how much. Of course, there has to be someone to chase them so here comes <b>Mary</b> the army medic who has been hunting for Gambol since time immemorial.<br/><br/>Notice the names: Jimmy is the main protagonist and Jimmy is a "common name." </span>
  <span id="freeText16551817566972004594" style="display:none">Bakerfield, California. <b>Jimmy Luntz</b> works for <b>Gambol</b> who in turn works for <b>Juarez.</b> Luntz loves a beautiful alcoholic Indian girl called <b>Anita Desilvera.</b> They are all petty thieves. One time, they got a big haul from a bank: $2.5M. This book is about greed and who should get how much. Of course, there has to be someone to chase them so here comes <b>Mary</b> the army medic who has been hunting for Gambol since time immemorial.<br /><br />Notice the names: Jimmy is the main protagonist and Jimmy is a "common name." The villains have Mexican names. The army medic chasing the bad guys has an American name. I don't want to nitpick but this stereotyping is just too predictable.<br /><br />Denis Johnson won the National Book Award for Fiction in 2007 for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/271074.Tree_of_Smoke" title="Tree of Smoke by Denis Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Tree of Smoke</a> (3 stars). That book was also one of the Pulitzer finalists for that year. I liked that book so I picked up this one.<br /><br />I am not really disappointed. The clipped gritty dialogues that I liked in his <i>Tree of Smoke</i> are also here. You know, for almost a month now, I am into completing all the available (I am in Manila so this is a challenge) books by Truman Capote. So, all this <i>gayish</i> (but fun, don't get me wrong) language is now a normal reading fare. The most disturbing so far is when he said in his <i>Answered Prayers</i> this line: <i>"Your mouthwatering dick..."</i> Huh? I was like, how can a dick be mouthwatering? So, reading this manly book by Johnson was like a good break from reading Capote and it helped me (for a while since I am still into that Capote quest) to bring back to my senses. The book is peppered with <i>"Fuck!"</i> or its longer version <i>"Fuck, if I know!"</i> that was also there in his award-winning <i>Tree.</i> All the while, Johnson was cleansing my funny thoughts about Capote until I got to this another disturbing line. This time courtesy of Johnson: <i>"I will cut your balls and if you die, it is your own decision."</i> Huh? <br /><br />It's funny how male novelists can make use of their own genitalia to spice up what I thought would have otherwise been boring works. Maybe because <i>Answered Prayers</i> was first serialized in <i>Esquire</i> and <i>Nobody Move</i> in <i>Playboy</i> and both of them are male magazines?<br /><br />It's different in Anne Enright's case though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16551817566972004594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3568588745" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3568588745" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3568588745?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312429614" href="/book/show/7291359-nobody-move">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3568588745" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1003756380304178129"><br/>I've been a big fan of Denis Johnson ever since being blown away by his short story collection Jesus' Son, and have been impressed with some of his other novels too, but this run-of-the-mill crime novel is seriously lacking in what made me really like him in the first place. It's not a bad novel, and is really dialogue-heavy, but it feels like I've come across the same formula a million times before - whether that be TV, film or book.<br/><br/>There is a large sum of money involved.<br/>There are gangsters, lo</span>
  <span id="freeText1003756380304178129" style="display:none"><br />I've been a big fan of Denis Johnson ever since being blown away by his short story collection Jesus' Son, and have been impressed with some of his other novels too, but this run-of-the-mill crime novel is seriously lacking in what made me really like him in the first place. It's not a bad novel, and is really dialogue-heavy, but it feels like I've come across the same formula a million times before - whether that be TV, film or book.<br /><br />There is a large sum of money involved.<br />There are gangsters, lowlifes, and those cheep motels that have neon sings that flicker.<br />We have a kind of anti-hero, and a sort of Femme fatale. <br />There is violence, which was like a mix of the Coen brothers and Quentin Tarantino.<br />It takes a lot from the hardboiled noir of Raymond Chandler and James M. Cain to name two.<br /><br />So, is it original? No.<br /><br />Did I enjoy it? Yes and no.<br /><br />It was a quick and easy read that I blitzed through in under a day, and thought the best thing about it was less about the characters: although they are presented well enough, and more about Johnson's descriptions of the bars, the booze, the small town streets, the motels, and the drugstores, which really captured a certain mood. Fans of crime fiction I'm sure would get more out of it than me, and it's not the sort of novel I'd read nowadays, but I did because it was Denis Johnson. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1003756380304178129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18146916189226062393">If this was written by Graham Greene it would have been subtitled an “entertainment”. A black humored noir with outrageously great dialogue and little of Johnson’s druggy weirdness, deep rooted melancholy and poetic overload (though much of the writing is still beautiful).   <span id="freeText18146916189226062393" style="display:none">If this was written by Graham Greene it would have been subtitled an “entertainment”. Johnson has used noir tropes before but never purely for the purpose of creating this much malicious fun. A black humored noir with outrageously great dialogue and little of Johnson’s druggy weirdness, deep rooted melancholy and poetic overload (though much of the writing is still beautiful). Fans of the Coen Brothers, and McCarthy and Pynchon recent decisions to write full throttled noir ( I wish more literary heavyweights would change it up like this, other examples are Stone’s Dog Soldiers, Delillo’s Running Dog, Faulkner’s Sanctuary, and off course Greene.) should eat this up. You can read it in an afternoon.</span>

<div id="review_1022656582" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1022656582" />
    <a title="Taylor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32865-taylor"><img alt="Taylor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567607577p2/32865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1022656582?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taylor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taylor" href="/user/show/32865-taylor">Taylor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Taylor by: </span>
      Vince
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=murders-and-mysteries">murders-and-mysteries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=wild-wild-west">wild-wild-west</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=book-bender">book-bender</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=ramble-on">ramble-on</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=make-em-laugh">make-em-laugh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=recommended">recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=lent">lent</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1022656582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5369301002183085178">Original review: If I was grading this on the scale that I apply to a lot of books, I might give it something closer to a three. But the thing is, I don't think there's an easy way to have a scale that applies to all books. I'm sure some people do. They have their criteria and they work from that, and maybe for all intents and purposes, that suits them just fine. For me, I'll admit, there's plenty that goes into that star rating up there, even if it seems that all I ever do is rate books a four </span>
  <span id="freeText5369301002183085178" style="display:none">Original review: If I was grading this on the scale that I apply to a lot of books, I might give it something closer to a three. But the thing is, I don't think there's an easy way to have a scale that applies to all books. I'm sure some people do. They have their criteria and they work from that, and maybe for all intents and purposes, that suits them just fine. For me, I'll admit, there's plenty that goes into that star rating up there, even if it seems that all I ever do is rate books a four - but ultimately, it's a gut feeling. There are a lot of fours for me in this world - books that I love and enjoy but that I'm hesitant to call perfect - but I enjoy and love a lot of books, even still. I'll be the first to admit that I'm fairly easy to please.<br /><br /><i>Nobody Move</i> is a fairly straight-forward caper about a man and woman who cross paths and are - perhaps not so coincidentally - both in a tight spot with varying types of law and/or its enforcement, as it were. There's not a ton here that you're going to be able to <i>analyze</i> or that's going to strike on an emotional fault and render you incapable of human interaction for a time. That's just not this book, and for me those are the kinds of books that tend to get the higher ratings.<br /><br />And yet...<br /><br />Johnson has this way of giving you little threads to yank at, to see if the tapestry comes undone to reveal something else hidden behind it. While the funny part about <i>Nobody Move</i> is that, despite the title, everyone is pretty much constantly in motion, much of the story takes place around a river that has this River Styx boundary between life and death thing going on, and much like the characters, you're drawn to that river, to know what it looks like, to see its color and its depth.<br /><br /><i>Nobody Move</i> is filled with details that, like that river, set it apart from stories that might otherwise feel similar. Gay criminals, a mobster whose display of power is to chop off a guy's balls and eat them. It's weird, dark, and funny in a way that makes me want to see a film version of this as directed by Quentin Tarantino. Seriously, Johnson's agent, please pitch this. It'd be perfect.<br /><br />I haven't read a lot of noir - though I'd like to - so I can't say how it stacks up to the standards or classics of the genre, but I can say that, as compared to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/835984850?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Train Dreams</i>
</a>, it's clear that Johnson can write in whatever the hell kind of way he wishes. Where <i>Train Dreams</i> was light and ethereal, <i>Nobody Moves</i> is gritty and harsh. One thing both have in common is Johnson's dark humor, which thus far hits that perfect spot of making you feel bad for laughing, and yet, not <i>too</i> bad.<br /><br />Mostly, <i>Nobody Move</i> is fun, and at the time, I needed a fun read. I've read a lot of books I've enjoyed lately, but nothing that was fun in the way that I wanted, hoped, or needed it to be. This is a quick and dirty job for when you've got a plane ride or an afternoon to kill and you're procrastinating on reading your book club book for the month, or cleaning your house, or whatever else it is you might be not totally dedicated to doing at that exact moment.<br /><br />Nobody makes me feel the way Steinbeck does, but the best thing I can say for what I've read of Johnson thus far is that he comes close to that place, where a circle and an arrow has been drawn on all of humanity's flaws and it makes us feel both worse but also better for them, somehow. He just doesn't quite hit that emotional depth, or at least, he hasn't for me, yet. But it's there, even if it's not as potent. If the rest of it wasn't enough (though it probably would be), for that alone, I'll look for more by him.<br /><br />-------<br /><br />Second read review:<br /><br />I was a bit nervous going into the second read on <i>Nobody Move</i>, because suspenseful reads can sometimes take a huge hit on another go 'round when you already know what's coming.<br /><br />What struck me on my second go through, however, was how truly wonderful all of the characters are, and how happy I was to be spending time with them again. Johnson has done an incredible job of creating people that both feel completely plausible but also a little more unique and interesting than the average Joe Schmo. He also does a credit to the phrase "easy to read" in that this book is proof that that doesn't have to be a derogatory term to mean "bad" or "simple" - there's depths and layers to <i>Nobody Move</i> without reading it feeling like a chore or a drag at any time, an impressive feat the more I think about it.<br /><br />I'll be curious to hear with the others in my book group think of it, but personally I fell in love with this book all over again, and can see this being a book that I'll continue to reread for the pure, indulgent pleasure of it. I also really hope that someone makes a movie out of it. It'd be such a great film in the right hands.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5369301002183085178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy Weston" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy Weston" href="/user/show/313206-andy-weston">Andy Weston</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/313206-andy-weston?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/313206-andy-weston?shelf=noir">noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/313206-andy-weston?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2068386759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1817009798085046510">This has a very powerful opening chapter and continues to be action-packed and thrilling throughout. Johnson's novel concerns Jimmy Luntz, and is as fine an example of American noir as I can think of, it has those key ingredients, seedy motel rooms, action on and off the freeways, and the slang dialogue. You are never sure who to empathise with, if anyone. Just as you start to warm to Jimmy he does something that makes you think the opposite. The other key characters, Juarez (who Jimmy owes mone</span>
  <span id="freeText1817009798085046510" style="display:none">This has a very powerful opening chapter and continues to be action-packed and thrilling throughout. Johnson's novel concerns Jimmy Luntz, and is as fine an example of American noir as I can think of, it has those key ingredients, seedy motel rooms, action on and off the freeways, and the slang dialogue. You are never sure who to empathise with, if anyone. Just as you start to warm to Jimmy he does something that makes you think the opposite. The other key characters, Juarez (who Jimmy owes money to), Gambol (who is hunting him), Mary, and Anita (who teams up with Jimmy), are wonderfully well described. <br /><br />Johnson is a master of including that bit of humour into his writing, yet managing to keep it so dark. I think that skill is the one that wins in the end, the one that makes Nobody Move stand out in its genre. <br /><br />Train Dreams stands apart from me as a Johnson novel. It is by far his best, yet can't be compared to something like this as they are so different. This is the best noir novel I have read by him, but luckily for me, I still have a few, including Tree of Smoke, to go. </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_47550594" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47550594" />
    <a title="Jason Pettus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus"><img alt="Jason Pettus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548377232p2/147289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47550594?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Pettus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Pettus" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus">Jason Pettus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47550594" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4258918818733139883">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com:]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted here illegally.)<br/><br/>As heavy readers know, it's common for authors of big, giant, important, award-winning tomes to follow them up with something light and short, for a variety of reasons: as a literary 'cleanser,' to avoid burnout as a writer, to pre-deflate high audience expectations. But this turns out to be a real h</span>
  <span id="freeText4258918818733139883" style="display:none">(Reprinted from the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com:]. I am the original author of this essay, as well as the owner of CCLaP; it is not being reprinted here illegally.)<br /><br />As heavy readers know, it's common for authors of big, giant, important, award-winning tomes to follow them up with something light and short, for a variety of reasons: as a literary 'cleanser,' to avoid burnout as a writer, to pre-deflate high audience expectations. But this turns out to be a real hit-and-miss proposition, also as heavy readers know; because sometimes you end up with, say, Michael Chabon's delightful genre experiment <i>Gentlemen of the Road</i> (his follow-up to the supposedly astounding <i>Yiddish Policemen's Union</i>, which believe it or not I still haven't gotten a chance to read), but then sometimes you end up with a book like Jonathan Lethem's truly dreadful <i>You Don't Love Me Yet</i> (his follow-up of sorts [there was a book of essays in between:] to the also supposedly astounding <i>Fortress of Solitude</i>, which I also still haven't gotten a chance yet to read).<br /><br />And now we have our latest example to judge, Denis Johnson's short pulp-fiction exercise <i>Nobody Move</i> (originally published serially last year in <i>Playboy</i>), his first book since the mindblowing 2007 National Book Award winning <i>Tree of Smoke</i> (which I've also reviewed here in the past, and whoo man what a phenomenal freaking book <i>that</i> is). And how is <i>Nobody Move</i>? Well, in a cliched nutshell: He shoots, he scores! And that's because Johnson does here what Chabon did as well, but Lethem simply did not -- he takes the light cleanser project just as seriously as he did the giant important award-winning one, even with them designed from the start to serve two very different purposes, honoring those intentions and taking a lot of care to get the details right. For example, just like most pulp projects, Johnson's novel is a look at a series of petty criminals and lowlife losers (in this case centered around the central California town of Bakersfield), which of course was one of the big things to originally differentiate the genre from the lurid crime tales of the Victorian Age that came right before it; that instead of featuring criminal masterminds or fiendish supervillains, the characters in pulp tales live out on the edges of society, too stupid and cowardly to go for the big score but rather sticking to the petty schemes they know definitively to work, trying to get away with them as long as possible without getting caught, while nonetheless always dreaming of the day their ship finally comes in.<br /><br />In this case, for example, there is the weasely schlub Jimmy Luntz, the closest thing we have to an 'antihero' if any of them can be called that; then there's the aging enforcer Gambol, who spends the book chasing Luntz after getting shot in the leg by him in the first five pages; there is Anita, the unusually attractive Native American alcoholic who has just gotten busted embezzling several million dollars from the company she works for (in actuality a frame-up by her ex-husband, plus the crooked judge who granted him a divorce), who drunkenly hooks up with Luntz while both are on the run; there is Juarez, Gambol's boss who Luntz screwed over not too long ago (hence Gambol being on his trail), a Middle Easterner who tells everyone he's Hispanic and who dresses like a gangsta rapper; and then there's Mary, a former army medic dispatched at the beginning of the story to go find Gambol and quietly patch him up, who just happens to be Juarez's ex-wife and who just happens to now hate him but needs his money. And then the thing that brings them all together is not much more than a MacGuffin, and not actually very important to the story at all (you know, like the glowing briefcase in Quentin Tarantino's <i>Pulp Fiction</i>), with the point instead to spend 200 pages watching these people all chase each other around, while spouting unbelievably great lines of dialogue at each other.<br /><br />Because that of course is one of the most well-known things about Johnson's writing style, what generates so many admirers but what you need to look out for as well; that if you're not already a fan of people like David Mamet, who attempt to boil stories down to the absolute minimum amount of words needed to make their point, you need to stay the hell away from Johnson no matter <i>how</i> much your friends keep recommending him, or else suffer a fit of self-righteous eye-rolling so bad that it will threaten to induce a seizure. And in fact Johnson delivers not only his usual brilliant yet controversial clipped dialogue here in <i>Nobody Move</i>, but even sometimes very cleverly skips over big sections of action-text when he thinks the audience doesn't need it; to cite one infamous example that I've already mentioned, how the opening scene of the book is of Gambol and Luntz riding in a car together, Gambol telling Luntz not to mess with the shotgun he's accidentally discovered in the back seat, while in the very next paragraph Gambol is now laying in the desert with a bullet in his leg, with it only then that we learn that he had been taking Luntz out somewhere desolated in order to do him some kind of unspecified harm.<br /><br />This is why fans of the genre love pulp fiction, after all, because it's storytelling taken to its most terse, rat-a-tat extreme; a meaty yet bare-boned way of telling a tale, like watching a couple of scrawny yet professional lightweight boxers duke it out, a chance to admire the literary arts at its most stripped-down and essential. And this is certainly the case with <i>Nobody Move</i>, with a series of developments that I won't divulge any more of but let's just say are always unexpected, funny and horrifying at the same time just like pulp fiction should be, held together with sparkling gritty dialogue and just the general scuminess of the entire milieu. And I don't have a lot more to say about it, actually, because frankly there isn't a lot more <i>to</i> say about it -- when all is said and done, it is nothing more than a genre tale, never once straying from the well-known tropes that define pulp fiction, which is why it's getting an above-average but not spectacular score today; yet is pulled off almost perfectly, which is why it gets a boost in its rating specifically for those who are existing fans of, say, Raymond Chandler. If you're the kind of person who likes reading only one or two pulp tales a year, this should be one of them; and of course for those who like the genre more than that, this title is absolutely not to be missed.<br /><br />Out of 10: <b>9.2</b>, or <b>9.9</b> for lovers of pulp fiction </span>
  <a data-text-id="4258918818733139883" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

For some reason, the opening pages reminded me of Richard Brautigan's writing, especially the tone, but then the story settles into a wicked noir stoked with double-crosses, violence, and black humor. </span>

<div id="review_149165858" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149165858" />
    <a title="Kirk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/667059-kirk"><img alt="Kirk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442453577p2/667059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149165858?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirk" href="/user/show/667059-kirk">Kirk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149165858" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7224647016031289298">Fun, quick, dirty---but don't mistake that for good or compelling. You definitely get a sense of Johnson slumming here. Well, maybe not slumming. How about channeling. Channeling testosterone. There's so much of it flowing through this noir diversion my back hair thickened before page 30. Because real men eat each other's testicles for lunch, see. They blow each other's melons open. The simple social graces are opportunities to cock-woggle. Ask a dude his name and he replies, "Fuck Off." Is that</span>
  <span id="freeText7224647016031289298" style="display:none">Fun, quick, dirty---but don't mistake that for good or compelling. You definitely get a sense of Johnson slumming here. Well, maybe not slumming. How about channeling. Channeling testosterone. There's so much of it flowing through this noir diversion my back hair thickened before page 30. Because real men eat each other's testicles for lunch, see. They blow each other's melons open. The simple social graces are opportunities to cock-woggle. Ask a dude his name and he replies, "Fuck Off." Is that Russian or Ukranian? Aw, who cares. Let's say "tits" a few times. It's fun and I can't do it either at work or church. Even the chicks in this book are tough! Oral sex is "a face full of fuckwad." There in a single line goes any bi-curious temptations my subconscious was entertaining. Another way in which I'm not John Cheever... The most sensitive guy in this book, of course, is a raging queen named Sol Fuchs. Or Sally Fucks to his friends. Haha--get it? Gay dudes are so gay.<br /><br />There are some funny moments. I laughed out loud when the chalk outline of a guy blown away on his knees was described. And the opening bit about singing in a men's chorus. (Sidenote: If Glee went noir, I might watch it). And the villain named The Tall Man who's worth a giggle because DJ Jazzy Johnson is, you know, Hammettin' it up. But really, by the end, you don't give a shit about the characters. Should you in noir? I don't know.... but I do know I'm sucker for that sense of poignancy that ends "The Maltese Falcon" or "The Drowing Pool." Christ, "The Drowing Pool"---the way it ends with that great scene of Archer and the local police dude knocking each other down until they stagger off in each other's arms, or Doc McCoy's deep but necessary sorrow at the inevitable marital adjument he must make in "The Getaway".... yeah, well, none of that fluffy stuff is here. This book is all stank, Hank. I'm not sure if it's a compliment or not that after reading it I wanted to go dig up a copy of "The Secret Life of Bees" to convince myself I had an iota of sensitivity left. (Don't worry. I got over the temptation. Real quick. Thanks, Maker's Mark....)<br /><br />I'm not ragging Denis Johnson. I love me some Denis Johnson. So much so I make a point not to spell it Dennis. It's like lovin' Poe enough not to spell it Allen. (Hope my students have that much love some day). I suspect this book was the quick crap DJ needed after a decade's constipation with Tree of Smoke. A little literary Milk of Magnesia, which is fine. At this point, DJ has earned the right to go wherever he wants and I'll ride. But let's face it, this isn't a homage to Hammett or Chandler; it's more of a Mickey Spillane with a chaser of self-aware wit. It's not even "Pulp Fiction"--more like From Dusk till Dawn. Indulgently dumb, almost daring you not to forget it. <br /><br />Again, I didn't dislike it. It was something to do on a Friday night that didn't involve a bail bondsman. But even as we praise grit and toughness we ought to interrogate this American fascination with ugly and venal. It may help us all feel we're not hopelessly bourgeois, but sometimes it's nice to think we're capable of feeling, too. <br /><br />Only not too much. Because that would make us pussies.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7224647016031289298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_132091138" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132091138" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/852867-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201672729p2/852867.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/132091138?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/852867-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132091138" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13248421439466658487">After the epic, messy sprawl of his last book, the ambitious yet uneven Vietnam novel Tree of Smoke, Denis Johnson returns to the spare leanness of his best work with this deliberately minor key modern noir. Like my three favorite Johnson books (Angels, Jesus' Son, and Already Dead: A California Gothic), this one is about unsavory characters and societal outcasts getting into trouble. Nobody Move is at once a modern updating of '40s hardboiled crime fiction with nods to Chandler and Hammett and </span>
  <span id="freeText13248421439466658487" style="display:none">After the epic, messy sprawl of his last book, the ambitious yet uneven Vietnam novel Tree of Smoke, Denis Johnson returns to the spare leanness of his best work with this deliberately minor key modern noir. Like my three favorite Johnson books (Angels, Jesus' Son, and Already Dead: A California Gothic), this one is about unsavory characters and societal outcasts getting into trouble. Nobody Move is at once a modern updating of '40s hardboiled crime fiction with nods to Chandler and Hammett and a violent story for our current economically ruinous times. Set in the small towns, rivers, and isolated stretches of highway near Bakersfield, California, the story follows a degenerate gambler and barbershop quartet member on the run from his bookie's thugs and his chance meeting with a woman who's just taken the fall for the millions she embezzled with her powerful ex-husband and his accomplice, a dying judge. Neither of them have been on the run before, and they don't know what they're doing. Bad things happen. I stayed up all night with a glass of bourbon, reading it. You should, too. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13248421439466658487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58703046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58703046" />
    <a title="Tyler Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2171826-tyler-jones"><img alt="Tyler Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310146343p2/2171826.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58703046?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler Jones" href="/user/show/2171826-tyler-jones">Tyler Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2171826-tyler-jones?shelf=hard-boiled-wonderland">hard-boiled-wonderland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58703046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15017867443508812981">"Nobody Move" is the Denis Johnson novel I have been waiting for since "The Name of the World" came out nearly ten years ago. For most of the interviening decade Johnson toiled away to produce "Tree of Smoke", a long-winded and often directionless novel. Enough people confused its massiveness for merit to earn Tree of Smoke the National Book Award - an honor that might have more to do with finally giving Johnson the credit he deserves for earlier (and better) works like "Resuscitation of a Hange</span>
  <span id="freeText15017867443508812981" style="display:none">"Nobody Move" is the Denis Johnson novel I have been waiting for since "The Name of the World" came out nearly ten years ago. For most of the interviening decade Johnson toiled away to produce "Tree of Smoke", a long-winded and often directionless novel. Enough people confused its massiveness for merit to earn Tree of Smoke the National Book Award - an honor that might have more to do with finally giving Johnson the credit he deserves for earlier (and better) works like "Resuscitation of a Hanged Man" or his brilliant first novel "Angels". How great it is then to see that Johnson is not letting the National Book Award go to his head. Only a mature writer who is confident in his own talent would follow up a critical triumph with a work that could easily be written off as fluff-fiction. <br /><br />"Nobody Move" is Johnson's version of the noir novel, complete with rabbit-punch prose, morally ambigous characters and violence in abundance. Johnson has always had a knack with characters who inhabit the perimeters of America (what another goodreads contributer perfectly sums up as "empathy for fuckheads"), so the underbelly of the crime world seems a natural fit. Similarly, the tight no-word-wasted style of noir fiction is perfectly suited to Johnson and the noir novels rather jaded world view wherein virtue and reward have no relationship is also a natural environment for Johnson, whose protagonists have never bought into the American myth of meritocracy.<br /><br />"Nobody Move" is smart, funny and compelling. It might be too entertaining to be considered a work of literature. Who cares? Give me more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15017867443508812981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/897540749?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allan MacDonell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allan MacDonell" href="/user/show/2456669-allan-macdonell">Allan MacDonell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer897540749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13930798653994186952">I’d seen <i>Nobody Move</i>—a palate-cleansing trifle (relatively speaking) from Denis Johnson—on bookstore shelves for around four years, and I consumed its 196 pages within half a day. The dark noir novel caters to an appetite for violence of the bloody style committed with caustic verbal flair in a skeeve-screw-skeeve kind of world. <i>Nobody Move</i> won’t strike the chords of existential dread ringing out from <i>Angels</i>; its environment is not the deeply rendered and disturbed alternative Eden of <i>Already De</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13930798653994186952" style="display:none">I’d seen <i>Nobody Move</i>—a palate-cleansing trifle (relatively speaking) from Denis Johnson—on bookstore shelves for around four years, and I consumed its 196 pages within half a day. The dark noir novel caters to an appetite for violence of the bloody style committed with caustic verbal flair in a skeeve-screw-skeeve kind of world. <i>Nobody Move</i> won’t strike the chords of existential dread ringing out from <i>Angels</i>; its environment is not the deeply rendered and disturbed alternative Eden of <i>Already Dead</i> or the cosmos of confusion navigated in <i>Resuscitation of a Hanged Man</i>. The book won’t induce <i>Tree of Smoke</i>’s epic historical hangover or <i>Jesus’ Son</i>’s aftereffect of mournful nostalgia, to name a few of my favorite Denis Johnson things. But before this quick book is closed for good, <i>Nobody Move</i> scrambles with the effervescent pleasure of a delight that is gone almost as soon as you taste it, a literary amuse-bouche that only a dope would spit out or criticize on grounds of insufficient calories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13930798653994186952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14368506634115933027">Here's the thing about Denis Johnson:<br/><br/>I don't love any single book he's written but I really love him as a writer. He's super versatile and does all kinds of styles and genres, but none of his attempts--that I've read yet--has been the kind of book that I can't imagine living without. Of course, I'd also recommend every single book I've read by him, and this is no exception.<br/><br/>It's hilarious, it's violent, it's complex, it's seductive, it's mysterious, and it's just fun.   <span id="freeText14368506634115933027" style="display:none">Here's the thing about Denis Johnson:<br /><br />I don't love any single book he's written but I really love him as a writer. He's super versatile and does all kinds of styles and genres, but none of his attempts--that I've read yet--has been the kind of book that I can't imagine living without. Of course, I'd also recommend every single book I've read by him, and this is no exception.<br /><br />It's hilarious, it's violent, it's complex, it's seductive, it's mysterious, and it's just fun. Johnson's take on noir is pretty interesting and reminds me of Takeshi Kitano (Sonatine) mixed with Martin McDonagh (Seven Psychopaths). <br /><br />But, yeah, I liked it. Definitely worth checking out.<br /><br />Also, the last three Johnson books I've gone through have been as audiobooks, and they've all been narrated by the same guy. I just think it's worth mentioning that he's an awesome and versatile narrator. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10477595696166346162">A lean little crime novel, that I'm giving 5 stars (genre rating). No big messages here (though I toyed with the idea that Johnson was saying something regarding Mercy, but then backed away from that, since such pondering messes with the mean fun of the novel). It is what it is. A cast of (well drawn) losers, all hooking for the big score. Not one ounce of fat, and great dialogue throughout. It reminded me a lot of a really good Leonard novel. Anita Desilvera is a great character (with some of t</span>
  <span id="freeText10477595696166346162" style="display:none">A lean little crime novel, that I'm giving 5 stars (genre rating). No big messages here (though I toyed with the idea that Johnson was saying something regarding Mercy, but then backed away from that, since such pondering messes with the mean fun of the novel). It is what it is. A cast of (well drawn) losers, all hooking for the big score. Not one ounce of fat, and great dialogue throughout. It reminded me a lot of a really good Leonard novel. Anita Desilvera is a great character (with some of the best lines), and one I wouldn't mind seeing again in a future novel, though I doubt that's going to happen, since <i>Nobody Move</i> reads like a one and (wonderfully) done effort. </span>

<div id="review_576165394" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/576165394" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1884982-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/576165394?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/1884982-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1884982-adam?shelf=1970-present">1970-present</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1884982-adam?shelf=prose">prose</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer576165394" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8554443014426519576">I think it's pretty awesome that a hugely acclaimed literary fiction writer opted to pay homage to the great American genre of the hardboiled crime novel, and not with any pomo-type spin or intent to subvert involved. But <i>Nobody Move</i> shows why this doesn't happen much, and shouldn't. Johnson knows how to write these sorts of characters, and he's got the dialogue exactly right. There's some pretty great writing here, fucking brilliant turns of phrase now and then. But Johnson doesn't have the gen</span>
  <span id="freeText8554443014426519576" style="display:none">I think it's pretty awesome that a hugely acclaimed literary fiction writer opted to pay homage to the great American genre of the hardboiled crime novel, and not with any pomo-type spin or intent to subvert involved. But <i>Nobody Move</i> shows why this doesn't happen much, and shouldn't. Johnson knows how to write these sorts of characters, and he's got the dialogue exactly right. There's some pretty great writing here, fucking brilliant turns of phrase now and then. But Johnson doesn't have the genre writer's knack for plot. I don't so much care about plot in literary fiction, especially if it's a 'novel of ideas' type deal, focused more on characters, but in a short crime novel with scant room to develop characters, there better be a hook, even if it's an elaborate macguffin ala Chandler or Hammett. There isn't, and the characters aren't strong enough to hold the book together. The prose is the most interesting aspect of this novel. It's very well-written, but the content is minimally worthwhile. <br /><br />Also, despite the promotional material referring to Chandler and Hammett a lot, this comes off more as a literary writer's take on Tarantino, or a slightly less punchy Elmore Leonard. So don't expect what you won't get. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8554443014426519576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/180643-jim-coughenour?shelf=darkandfunny">darkandfunny</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55644846" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17358216050232621129">This book's a bright disappointment, a <i>Playboy</i> pastiche of Elmore Leonard. It has sticky passages of hilarity, barbed dialogue as punctured as epigrams, and the usual motley minor misfits entangled by emotional aphasia and pluck. The plot's shot full of holes, sliding off the page like brainbits of a murdered lover, rolling into corners like severed testicles – and what remains by the end manages, after a few raw chortles, to strangle itself with its own colostomy bag.<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16945927604489256749">Since I had never read Denis Johnson before, and this book was quite short, it seemed an excellent way to check his work out. Knowing his reputation as a serious novelist with awards for his work, I did not expect this fast-paced and entertaining update of pulp fiction. The book was originally serialized in four parts in Playboy, which does not interrupt the propulsive narrative. Jimmy and Anita are a couple of down-and-out characters dealings with debt collectors, crooked ex-spouses and other a</span>
  <span id="freeText16945927604489256749" style="display:none">Since I had never read Denis Johnson before, and this book was quite short, it seemed an excellent way to check his work out. Knowing his reputation as a serious novelist with awards for his work, I did not expect this fast-paced and entertaining update of pulp fiction. The book was originally serialized in four parts in Playboy, which does not interrupt the propulsive narrative. Jimmy and Anita are a couple of down-and-out characters dealings with debt collectors, crooked ex-spouses and other assorted lowlifes offered by the city of Bakersfield, California. Their twists and turns make for a narrative that never lets up. A great read for anyone who misses the pulps of olden days.</span>

<div id="review_62835503" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62835503" />
    <a title="stacy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/138336-stacy"><img alt="stacy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182222122p2/138336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62835503?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="stacy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="stacy" href="/user/show/138336-stacy">stacy</a>
      </span>

          added it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62835503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6792584962885148196">Of Johnson's work, <i>Jesus' Son</i> is my favorite and <i>Nobody Move</i> is my most recent. I'll have to read a second time to confirm my suspicion that Johnson's humor is bone-deep as is his love for loser protagonists. They come out equal parts do-gooder and total fuck-up.<br/><br/>This book comes off as a quick and dirty assignment, something to play at, after years of toil on <i>Tree of Smoke</i>. And I'm still trying to figure its introductory remarks on war.<br/><br/>Anyway, there are plenty of lines to love in <i>Nobody Move</i>. Her</span>
  <span id="freeText6792584962885148196" style="display:none">Of Johnson's work, <i>Jesus' Son</i> is my favorite and <i>Nobody Move</i> is my most recent. I'll have to read a second time to confirm my suspicion that Johnson's humor is bone-deep as is his love for loser protagonists. They come out equal parts do-gooder and total fuck-up.<br /><br />This book comes off as a quick and dirty assignment, something to play at, after years of toil on <i>Tree of Smoke</i>. And I'm still trying to figure its introductory remarks on war.<br /><br />Anyway, there are plenty of lines to love in <i>Nobody Move</i>. Here are a few of my faves:<br /><br />"Early in his teens Luntz had fought Golden Gloves. Clumsy in the ring, he'd distinguished himself the wrong way--the only boy to get knocked out twice. He'd spent two years at it. His secret was that he'd never, before or since, felt so comfortable or so at home as when lying on his back and listening to the far-off music of the referee's ten-count." (p 31)<br /><br />Johnson has a thing for music. He finds all kinds of ways in his works to pass off his love for it.<br /><br />"She came to him with a hypo and said, 'The cavalry has arrived.' By this time the pain had conquered every physical part of him and had begun to involve his soul. Then the sensations flattened out and got hard to locate, and as long as he didn't try moving, things were pretty jolly." (p 46)<br /><br />"Gambol closed his eyes. He felt his head nodding forward and rode a Ferris wheel down into violent cartoons." (p 60)<br /><br />This guy. What a love for words and play.<br /><br />"She picked out a JCPenny pantsuit, a JCPenny blouse, a JCPenny skirt, and the best underwear they had, which wasn't much. While Jimmy stood around waiting for her she sat in the dressing room momentarily naked with these latest humiliations at her feet and rage in her heart. JCPenney." (pp 65-66)<br /><br />Sweet anger is this man's specialty. It turns out ugly, but his rendering of it glimmers. Plus I always felt the same way about JCPenney. Then again, it was before this ad came out: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://gawker.com/396843/teen-sex-gains-mainstream-approval-with-jc-penney-ad">http://gawker.com/396843/teen-sex-gai...</a>.<br /><br />"The TV emitted a small steady roar. In the show a man clung to the side of a speeding train. Luntz let the TV run so he could see her by its light. All through their lovemaking Anita kept quiet, but she looked right at him. When she came, she said, 'No. No. No.'" (p 84)<br /><br />I'm not sure if there exists a better way to render Anita. It's all right there. And Jimmy, too. Sad "lucky" sap.<br /><br /><br />There's more. Read it for yourself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6792584962885148196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_51926309" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51926309" />
    <a title="Brittany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1270609-brittany"><img alt="Brittany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320240494p2/1270609.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51926309?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brittany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brittany" href="/user/show/1270609-brittany">Brittany</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Coen brothers / film noir</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Brittany by: </span>
      Harper C
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1270609-brittany?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1270609-brittany?shelf=advance-reader-edition">advance-reader-edition</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1270609-brittany?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51926309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2785645861704684014">How I Came To Read This Book: Harper Collins sent it to me woo.<br/><br/>The Plot: Jimmy Luntz is a grifter whose bad luck catches up with him when a man named Gambol shows up to collect on Luntz's gambling debts. Rather than take things lying down, Luntz ends up shooting Gambol and leaving him for dead - and soon it's a high-octane game of cat and mouse with Luntz on the run (with a sexy con artist in tow) and the mafia at his heels. <br/><br/>The Good & The Bad: I can't call this book mass market or literary fict</span>
  <span id="freeText2785645861704684014" style="display:none">How I Came To Read This Book: Harper Collins sent it to me woo.<br /><br />The Plot: Jimmy Luntz is a grifter whose bad luck catches up with him when a man named Gambol shows up to collect on Luntz's gambling debts. Rather than take things lying down, Luntz ends up shooting Gambol and leaving him for dead - and soon it's a high-octane game of cat and mouse with Luntz on the run (with a sexy con artist in tow) and the mafia at his heels. <br /><br />The Good &amp; The Bad: I can't call this book mass market or literary fiction. It's very much a Coen brothers film in book form - which has its pros and cons. As a note, that will totally make you biased, this book was originally published (in four parts I presume, based on the book set-up) in the pages of Playboy. So yes, there is some gratuitous sex here and there, but it's mostly pretty tame and on par with the heightened level of drama and tension throughout. Another weakness would be that a huge part of the book takes place in back and forth dialogue, which makes it hard to decipher who exactly is talking. Overall though, this is a snappy book tinged with dark comedy. It's not particularly deep, but it's written well enough that I wouldn't call it a piece of mass market fiction.<br /><br />The Bottom Line: Coen Brothers - on paper.<br /><br />Anything Memorable? No.<br /><br />50-Book Challenge?: Book #15 in 2009.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2785645861704684014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_104822706" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/104822706" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1336647-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216254350p2/1336647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/104822706?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/1336647-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer104822706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6086985874343442507">While the experts may claim there is no such thing as a perfect crime, Nobody Move is a perfect crime novel - full of moral mazes, messy murders, and topped with a dark and drunken femme fatale. Perhaps the most exciting element is Johnson's subtle and continuous probing into the inner depths of his characters - a significant plot point all too often dropped by strength of the inherent limits of the genre. The sentence-by-sentence quality of his words is unrelenting. And, since this is Denis Joh</span>
  <span id="freeText6086985874343442507" style="display:none">While the experts may claim there is no such thing as a perfect crime, Nobody Move is a perfect crime novel - full of moral mazes, messy murders, and topped with a dark and drunken femme fatale. Perhaps the most exciting element is Johnson's subtle and continuous probing into the inner depths of his characters - a significant plot point all too often dropped by strength of the inherent limits of the genre. The sentence-by-sentence quality of his words is unrelenting. And, since this is Denis Johnson, the ending is a deft mix of a Kafkaesque punchline and moral legerdemain. <br /><br />I will probably read Nobody Move a second time. In light of the ending, the relationship between the plot and the characters is complex enough to merit a second pass for additional clues. This complexity is fascinating when taken in the light of the major criticism of the whodunit narrative; namely that they withstand only a single read - like a joke - never to be interesting again. Nobody Moves manages to deftly trump this charge. It will remain a lasting piece of America's single contribution to the catalog of genre: the crime novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6086985874343442507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
